class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-Panspermia rootpage-Panspermia skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Panspermia</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Hypothesis that life exists throughout the Universe, distributed by space dust, meteoroids, asteroids, comets, planetoids, and also by spacecraft carrying unintended contamination by microorganisms</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panspermie.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Panspermie.svg/300px-Panspermie.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Panspermie.svg/450px-Panspermie.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Panspermie.svg/600px-Panspermie.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panspermie.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panspermia proposes that bodies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a> transported life forms such as <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>&#8212;complete with their <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>&#8212;through space to the Earth</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Panspermia</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i>&#960;&#8118;&#957;<i> (pan)</i>, meaning \'all\', and <i> </i>&#963;&#960;&#941;&#961;&#956;&#945;<i> (sperma)</i>, meaning \'seed\') is the <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> that <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> exists throughout the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, distributed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_dust" title="Space dust">space dust</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARX-20171106_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARX-20171106-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA-20180110_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-SA-20180110-2">[2]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cometary_panspermia_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-cometary_panspermia-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">planetoids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and also by <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> carrying unintended <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forward-contamination" title="Forward-contamination">contamination</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140519_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140519-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131106_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131106-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHYS-20180227_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHYS-20180227-7">[7]</a></sup> Distribution may have occurred between <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, and so may not be restricted to the limited scale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_systems" title="Solar systems">solar systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBC-20181026_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-NBC-20181026-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNRAS-20181011_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-MNRAS-20181011-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Panspermia hypotheses propose (for example) that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microscopic_life" title="Microscopic life">microscopic life-forms</a> that can survive the effects of space (such as <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophiles</a>) can become trapped in debris ejected into space after collisions between planets and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_bodies" title="Small Solar System bodies">small Solar System bodies</a> that harbor life. Some <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> may travel dormant for an extended amount of time before colliding randomly with other planets or intermingling with <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disks</a>. Under certain ideal impact circumstances (into a body of water, for example), and ideal conditions on a new planet\'s surfaces, it is possible that the surviving organisms could become active and begin to colonize their new environment. Panspermia studies concentrate not on  <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">how life began</a>, but on the methods that may cause its distribution in the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wesson_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wesson-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoyle,_F_1981._pp35-49_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoyle,_F_1981._pp35-49-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wickramasinghe,_J._2010._pp_137-154_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wickramasinghe,_J._2010._pp_137-154-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pseudo-panspermia (sometimes called <i>"soft panspermia"</i> or <i>"molecular panspermia"</i>) argues that the pre-biotic organic building-blocks of life originated in space, became incorporated in the solar nebula from which planets condensed, and were further&#8212;and continuously&#8212;distributed to planetary surfaces where life then emerged (<a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klyce_2001_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klyce_2001-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-14">[14]</a></sup> From the early 1970s, it started to become evident that interstellar dust included a large component of organic molecules. Interstellar molecules are formed by chemical reactions within very sparse interstellar or circumstellar clouds of dust and gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas103_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas103-15">[15]</a></sup> The dust plays a critical role in shielding the molecules from the ionizing effect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> emitted by stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_pais95_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown_pais95-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The chemistry leading to <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> may have begun shortly after the Big Bang, 13.8 billion years ago, during a habitable epoch when the Universe was only 10 to 17 million years old. Though the presence of life is confirmed only on the Earth, some scientists think that extraterrestrial life is not only plausible, but probable or inevitable. Probes and instruments have started examining other planets and moons in the Solar System and in other planetary systems for evidence of having once supported simple life, and projects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SETI" title="SETI">SETI</a> attempt to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_transmission" title="Radio transmission">radio transmissions</a> from possible extraterrestrial civilizations.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a <span class="toctext">Proposed mechanisms</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Radiopanspermia"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Radiopanspermia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Lithopanspermia"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Lithopanspermia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Accidental_panspermia"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Accidental panspermia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Directed_panspermia"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Directed panspermia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Pseudo-panspermia"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Pseudo-panspermia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Extraterrestrial_life"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Extraterrestrial life</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Hypotheses_on_extraterrestrial_sources_of_illnesses"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Hypotheses on extraterrestrial sources of illnesses</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Case_studies"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Case studies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Hoaxes"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Hoaxes</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Extremophiles"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Extremophiles</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Research_in_outer_space"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Research in outer space</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-14"><a href="#ERA"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">ERA</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#BIOPAN"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">BIOPAN</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#EXOSTACK"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">EXOSTACK</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-17"><a href="#EXPOSE"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.4</span> <span class="toctext">EXPOSE</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Tanpopo"><span class="tocnumber">4.1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Tanpopo</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Criticism"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The first known mention of the term was in the writings of the 5th-century BC <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Panspermia began to assume a more scientific form through the proposals of <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="J&#246;ns Jacob Berzelius">J&#246;ns Jacob Berzelius</a> (1834),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Hermann E. Richter (1865),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Kelvin</a> (1871),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a> (1879)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and finally reaching the level of a detailed scientific <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> through the efforts of the Swedish chemist <a href="/wiki/Svante_Arrhenius" title="Svante Arrhenius">Svante Arrhenius</a> (1903).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a> (1915&#8211;2001) and <a href="/wiki/Chandra_Wickramasinghe" title="Chandra Wickramasinghe">Chandra Wickramasinghe</a> (born 1939) were influential proponents of panspermia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollination_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pollination-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panspermia_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panspermia-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1974 they proposed the hypothesis that some <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust in interstellar space</a> was largely <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> (containing carbon), which Wickramasinghe later proved to be correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Hoyle and Wickramasinghe further contended that life forms continue to enter the Earth\'s atmosphere, and may be responsible for epidemic outbreaks, new diseases, and the genetic novelty necessary for <a href="/wiki/Macroevolution" title="Macroevolution">macroevolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In an Origins Symposium presentation on April 7, 2009, physicist <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a> stated his opinion about what humans may find when venturing into space, such as the possibility of alien life through the theory of panspermia: "Life could spread from planet to planet or from stellar system to stellar system, carried on meteors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephen_Hawking_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephen_Hawking-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Three series of astrobiology experiments have been conducted outside the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> between 2008 and 2015 (<a href="/wiki/EXPOSE" title="EXPOSE">EXPOSE</a>) where a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a>, microorganisms, and their spores were exposed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flux" title="Solar flux">solar flux</a> and vacuum of space for about 1.5 years. Some organisms survived in an inactive state for considerable lengths of time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and those samples sheltered by simulated meteorite material provide experimental evidence for the likelihood of the hypothetical scenario of lithopanspermia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several simulations in laboratories and in low Earth orbit suggest that ejection, entry and impact is survivable for some simple organisms. In 2015, remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> were found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>, when the <a href="/wiki/Early_Earth" title="Early Earth">young Earth</a> was about 400 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-35">[35]</a></sup> According to one researcher, "If life arose relatively quickly on Earth &#8230; then it could be common in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2018, a Russian team published a paper which disclosed that they found DNA on the exterior of the ISS from land and marine bacteria similar to those previously observed in superficial micro layers at the Barents and Kara seas\' coastal zones. They conclude "The presence of the wild land and marine bacteria DNA on the ISS suggests their possible transfer from the stratosphere into the ionosphere with the ascending branch of the <a href="/wiki/Global_atmospheric_electrical_circuit" title="Global atmospheric electrical circuit">global atmospheric electrical circuit</a>. Alternatively, the wild land and marine bacteria as well as the ISS bacteria may all have an ultimate space origin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russia_ISS_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russia_ISS-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2018, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a> astronomers presented an <a href="/wiki/Analysis" title="Analysis">analytical model</a> that suggests matter&#8212;and potentially dormant <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>&#8212;can be exchanged across the vast distances between <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, a process termed \'galactic panspermia\', and not be restricted to the limited scale of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NBC-20181026_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-NBC-20181026-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNRAS-20181011_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-MNRAS-20181011-9">[9]</a></sup> The detection of an extra-solar object named <a href="/wiki/%CA%BBOumuamua" title="&#699;Oumuamua">&#699;Oumuamua</a> crossing the inner Solar System in a\nhyperbolic orbit confirms the existence of a continuing material link with exoplanetary systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_mechanisms">Proposed mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Proposed mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Panspermia can be said to be either interstellar (between <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">star systems</a>) or interplanetary (between <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planets in the same star system</a>);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT-20140308_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAT-20140308-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140306_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140306-39">[39]</a></sup> its transport mechanisms may include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comets_and_Panspermia_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comets_and_Panspermia-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comets_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comets-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> and lithopanspermia (microorganisms embedded in rocks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mileikowsky_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mileikowsky-44">[44]</a></sup> Interplanetary transfer of nonliving material is well documented, as evidenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars_meteorite" title="Mars meteorite">meteorites of Martian origin</a> found on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mileikowsky_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mileikowsky-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">Space probes</a> may also be a viable transport mechanism for interplanetary cross-pollination in the Solar System or even beyond. However, space agencies have implemented <a href="/wiki/Planetary_protection" title="Planetary protection">planetary protection</a> procedures to reduce the risk of planetary contamination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> although, as recently discovered, some microorganisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Tersicoccus_phoenicis" title="Tersicoccus phoenicis">Tersicoccus phoenicis</a>, may be resistant to procedures used in <a href="/wiki/Cleanroom" title="Cleanroom">spacecraft assembly clean room facilities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20140519_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20140519-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131106_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131106-6">[6]</a></sup> In 2012, mathematician <a href="/wiki/Edward_Belbruno" title="Edward Belbruno">Edward Belbruno</a> and astronomers Amaya Moro-Mart&#237;n and Renu Malhotra proposed that gravitational <a href="/wiki/Low-energy_transfer" title="Low-energy transfer">low-energy transfer</a> of rocks among the young planets of stars in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_cluster" title="Birth cluster">birth cluster</a> is commonplace, and not rare in the general galactic stellar population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belbruno2012_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belbruno2012-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Deliberate directed panspermia from space to seed Earth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crick_Orgel_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crick_Orgel-49">[49]</a></sup> or sent from Earth to seed other <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary systems</a> have also been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-2011_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-2011-53">[53]</a></sup> One twist to the hypothesis by engineer Thomas Dehel (2006), proposes that <a href="/wiki/Plasmoid" title="Plasmoid">plasmoid</a> magnetic fields ejected from the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> may move the few spores lifted from the Earth\'s atmosphere with sufficient speed to cross interstellar space to other systems before the spores can be destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiopanspermia">Radiopanspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Radiopanspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1903, <a href="/wiki/Svante_Arrhenius" title="Svante Arrhenius">Svante Arrhenius</a> published in his article <i>The Distribution of Life in Space</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> the hypothesis now called radiopanspermia, that microscopic forms of life can be propagated in space, driven by the <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a> from stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Arrhenius argued that particles at a critical size below 1.5 &#956;m would be propagated at high speed by radiation pressure of the Sun. However, because its effectiveness decreases with increasing size of the particle, this mechanism holds for very tiny particles only, such as single bacterial spores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerda_Horneck_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gerda_Horneck-58">[58]</a></sup> The main criticism of radiopanspermia hypothesis came from <a href="/wiki/Iosif_Shklovsky" title="Iosif Shklovsky">Iosif Shklovsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>, who pointed out the proofs of the lethal action of space radiations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV" title="UV">UV</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>) in the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Regardless of the evidence, Wallis and Wickramasinghe argued in 2004 that the transport of individual bacteria or clumps of bacteria, is overwhelmingly more important than lithopanspermia in terms of numbers of microbes transferred, even accounting for the death rate of unprotected bacteria in transit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Then, data gathered by the orbital experiments <a href="/wiki/Exobiology_Radiation_Assembly" title="Exobiology Radiation Assembly">ERA</a>, <a href="/wiki/BIOPAN" title="BIOPAN">BIOPAN</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EXOSTACK" title="EXOSTACK">EXOSTACK</a> and <a href="/wiki/EXPOSE" title="EXPOSE">EXPOSE</a>, determined that isolated spores, including those of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B._subtilis" title="B. subtilis">B. subtilis</a></i>, were killed by several orders of magnitude if exposed to the full space environment for a mere few seconds, but if shielded against solar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV" title="UV">UV</a>, the spores were capable of surviving in space for up to six years while embedded in clay or meteorite powder (artificial meteorites).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerda_Horneck_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gerda_Horneck-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protection_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Protection-61">[61]</a></sup> Though minimal protection is required to shelter a spore against UV radiation, exposure to solar UV and cosmic ionizing radiation of unprotected DNA, break it up into its bases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transport_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-transport-64">[64]</a></sup> Also, exposing DNA to the ultrahigh vacuum of space alone is sufficient to cause DNA damage, so the transport of unprotected DNA or RNA during interplanetary flights powered solely by light pressure is extremely unlikely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transport_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-transport-64">[64]</a></sup> The feasibility of other means of transport for the more massive shielded spores into the outer Solar System &#8211; for example, through gravitational capture by comets &#8211; is at this time unknown.\n</p><p>Based on experimental data on radiation effects and DNA stability, it has been concluded that for such long travel times, boulder-sized rocks which are greater than or equal to 1 meter in diameter are required to effectively shield resistant microorganisms, such as bacterial spores against galactic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Clark_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-C._Clark-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> These results clearly negate the radiopanspermia hypothesis, which requires single spores accelerated by the radiation pressure of the Sun, requiring many years to travel between the planets, and support the likelihood of interplanetary transfer of microorganisms within <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> or <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, the so-called <b>lithopanspermia</b>  hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gerda_Horneck_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gerda_Horneck-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Protection_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Protection-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithopanspermia">Lithopanspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lithopanspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Lithopanspermia, the transfer of organisms in rocks from one planet to another either through interplanetary or interstellar space, remains speculative. Although there is no evidence that lithopanspermia has occurred in the Solar System, the various stages have become amenable to experimental testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_methods_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Experimental_methods-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Planetary ejection</b> &#8212; For lithopanspermia to occur, researchers have suggested that microorganisms must survive ejection from a planetary surface which involves extreme forces of acceleration and shock with associated temperature excursions. Hypothetical values of shock pressures experienced by ejected rocks are obtained with Martian meteorites, which suggest the shock pressures of approximately 5 to 55&#160;GPa, acceleration of 3&#160;Mm/s<sup>2</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jerk_(physics)" title="Jerk (physics)">jerk</a> of 6&#160;Gm/s<sup>3</sup> and post-shock temperature increases of about 1 K to 1000 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cockell_2008_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cockell_2008-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> To determine the effect of acceleration during ejection on microorganisms, rifle and ultracentrifuge methods were successfully used under simulated outer space conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_methods_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Experimental_methods-67">[67]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Survival in transit</b> &#8212; The survival of microorganisms has been studied extensively using both simulated facilities and in low Earth orbit. A large number of microorganisms have been selected for exposure experiments. It is possible to separate these microorganisms into two groups, the human-borne, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extremophiles" title="Extremophiles">extremophiles</a>. Studying the human-borne microorganisms is significant for human welfare and future manned missions; whilst the extremophiles are vital for studying the physiological requirements of survival in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_methods_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Experimental_methods-67">[67]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Atmospheric entry</b> &#8212; An important aspect of the lithopanspermia hypothesis to test is that microbes situated on or within rocks could survive hypervelocity entry from space through Earth\'s atmosphere (Cockell, 2008). As with planetary ejection, this is experimentally tractable, with sounding rockets and orbital vehicles being used for microbiological experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Experimental_methods_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Experimental_methods-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cockell_2008_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cockell_2008-68">[68]</a></sup> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B._subtilis" title="B. subtilis">B. subtilis</a></i> spores inoculated onto <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> domes were subjected to hypervelocity atmospheric transit (twice) by launch to a &#8764;120&#160;km altitude on an Orion two-stage rocket. The spores were shown to have survived on the sides of the rock, but they did not survive on the forward-facing surface that was subjected to a maximum temperature of 145&#160;&#176;C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In separate experiments, as part of the ESA STONE experiment, numerous organisms were embedded in different types or rocks and were mounted in the heat shield of six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foton_(space_programs)" title="Foton (space programs)">Foton re-entry capsules</a>. During reentry, the rock samples were subjected to temperatures and pressure loads comparable to those experienced in meteorites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The exogenous arrival of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> microorganisms could have quite profound consequences for the course of biological evolution on the inoculated planet. As photosynthetic organisms must be close to the surface of a rock to obtain sufficient light energy, atmospheric transit might act as a filter against them by ablating the surface layers of the rock. Although <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> have been shown to survive the desiccating, freezing conditions of space in orbital experiments, this would be of no benefit as the STONE experiment showed that they cannot survive atmospheric entry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-filter_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-filter-72">[72]</a></sup> Thus, non-photosynthetic organisms deep within rocks have a chance to survive the exit and entry process. (See also: <a href="/wiki/Impact_survival" title="Impact survival">Impact survival</a>.)  Research presented at the European Planetary Science Congress in 2015 suggests that ejection, entry and impact is survivable for some simple organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidental_panspermia">Accidental panspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Accidental panspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gold" title="Thomas Gold">Thomas Gold</a>, a professor of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, suggested in 1960 the hypothesis of "Cosmic Garbage", that life on Earth might have originated accidentally from a pile of <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">waste</a> products dumped on Earth long ago by extraterrestrial beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Directed_panspermia">Directed panspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Directed panspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Directed_panspermia" title="Directed panspermia">Directed panspermia</a></div>\n<p>Directed panspermia concerns the deliberate transport of microorganisms in space, sent to Earth to start life here, or sent from Earth to seed new planetary systems with life by <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a> of microorganisms on lifeless planets. The Nobel prize winner <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Orgel" title="Leslie Orgel">Leslie Orgel</a> proposed that life may have been purposely spread by an advanced extraterrestrial civilization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crick_Orgel_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crick_Orgel-49">[49]</a></sup> but considering an early "<a href="/wiki/RNA_world" title="RNA world">RNA world</a>" Crick noted later that life may have originated on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> It has been suggested that \'directed\' panspermia was proposed in order to counteract various objections, including the argument that microbes would be inactivated by the space environment and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_radiation" title="Cosmic radiation">cosmic radiation</a> before they could make a chance encounter with Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-C._Clark_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-C._Clark-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Conversely, active directed panspermia has been proposed to secure and expand life in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This may be motivated by biotic ethics that values, and seeks to propagate, the basic patterns of our organic gene/protein life-form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The panbiotic program would seed new planetary systems nearby, and clusters of new stars in interstellar clouds. These young targets, where local life would not have formed yet, avoid any interference with local life.\n</p><p>For example, microbial payloads launched by solar sails at speeds up to 0.0001 <i>c</i> (30,000&#160;m/s) would reach targets at 10 to 100 light-years in 0.1 million to 1 million years. Fleets of microbial capsules can be aimed at clusters of new stars in star-forming clouds, where they may land on planets or be captured by asteroids and comets and later delivered to planets. Payloads may contain <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophiles</a> for diverse environments and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> similar to early microorganisms. Hardy multicellular organisms (rotifer cysts) may be included to induce higher evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The probability of hitting the target zone can be calculated from <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle P({\\text{target}})={\\frac {A({\\text{target}})}{\\pi (dy)^{2}}}={\\frac {ar({\\text{target}})^{2}v^{2}}{(tp)^{2}d^{4}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>P</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>target</mtext>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>target</mtext>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>y</mi>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>a</mi>\n<mi>r</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>target</mtext>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mi>p</mi>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle P({\\text{target}})={\\frac {A({\\text{target}})}{\\pi (dy)^{2}}}={\\frac {ar({\\text{target}})^{2}v^{2}}{(tp)^{2}d^{4}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle P({\\text{target}})={\\frac {A({\\text{target}})}{\\pi (dy)^{2}}}={\\frac {ar({\\text{target}})^{2}v^{2}}{(tp)^{2}d^{4}}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73a2c85650deb4716d271c5e6e2ffe9dcab65d0c" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:40.535ex; height:6.676ex;"/></span> where <i>A</i>(target) is the cross-section of the target area, <i>dy</i> is the positional uncertainty at arrival; <i>a</i> &#8211; constant (depending on units), <i>r</i>(target) is the radius of the target area; <i>v</i> the velocity of the probe; (tp) the targeting precision (arcsec/yr); and <i>d</i> the distance to the target, guided by high-resolution astrometry of 1&#215;10<sup>&#8722;5</sup> arcsec/yr (all units in SIU). These calculations show that relatively near target stars(Alpha PsA, Beta Pictoris) can be seeded by milligrams of launched microbes; while seeding the Rho Ophiochus star-forming cloud requires hundreds of kilograms of dispersed capsules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Directed panspermia to secure and expand life in space is becoming possible because of developments in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_sails" title="Solar sails">solar sails</a>, precise <a href="/wiki/Astrometry" title="Astrometry">astrometry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planets" title="Extrasolar planets">extrasolar planets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extremophiles" title="Extremophiles">extremophiles</a> and microbial <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> After determining the composition of chosen meteorites, <a href="/wiki/Astroecology" title="Astroecology">astroecologists</a> performed laboratory experiments that suggest that many colonizing microorganisms and some plants could obtain many of their chemical nutrients from asteroid and cometary materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bioresources_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-bioresources-81">[81]</a></sup> However, the scientists noted that phosphate (PO<sub>4</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> (NO<sub>3</sub>&#8211;N) critically limit nutrition to many terrestrial lifeforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bioresources_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-bioresources-81">[81]</a></sup> With such materials, and energy from long-lived stars, microscopic life planted by directed panspermia could find an immense future in the galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of publications since 1979 have proposed the idea that directed panspermia could be demonstrated to be the origin of all life on Earth if a distinctive \'signature\' message were found, deliberately implanted into either the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">genetic code</a> of the first microorganisms by our hypothetical progenitor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_New_York_Times_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_New_York_Times-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn 2013 a team of physicists claimed that they had found mathematical and <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotic patterns</a> in the genetic code which they think is evidence for such a signature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redit_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-redit-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> This claim has been challenged by biologist <a href="/wiki/PZ_Myers" title="PZ Myers">PZ Myers</a> who said, writing in <a href="/wiki/Pharyngula_(blog)" title="Pharyngula (blog)">Pharyngula</a>: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Unfortunately, what they&#8217;ve so honestly described is good old honest garbage ... Their methods failed to recognize a well-known functional association in the genetic code; they did not rule out the operation of natural law before rushing to falsely infer design ... We certainly don&#8217;t need to invoke panspermia. Nothing in the genetic code requires design. and the authors haven&#8217;t demonstrated otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p> In a later peer-reviewed article, the authors address the operation of natural law in an extensive statistical test, and draw the same conclusion as in the previous article.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In special sections they also discuss methodological concerns raised by PZ Myers and some others.\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pseudo-panspermia">Pseudo-panspermia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pseudo-panspermia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_interstellar_and_circumstellar_molecules" title="List of interstellar and circumstellar molecules">List of interstellar and circumstellar molecules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis#Extraterrestrial_organic_molecules" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis &#167;&#160;Extraterrestrial organic molecules</a></div>\n<p>Pseudo-panspermia (sometimes called soft panspermia, molecular panspermia or quasi-panspermia) proposes that the organic molecules used for life originated in space and were incorporated in the solar nebula, from which the planets condensed and were further &#8212;and continuously&#8212; distributed to planetary surfaces where life then emerged (<a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klyce_2001_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Klyce_2001-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-14">[14]</a></sup> From the early 1970s it was becoming evident that interstellar dust consisted of a large component of organic molecules. The first suggestion came from <a href="/wiki/Chandra_Wickramasinghe" title="Chandra Wickramasinghe">Chandra Wickramasinghe</a>, who proposed a polymeric composition based on the molecule <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> (CH<sub>2</sub>O).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Interstellar molecules are formed by chemical reactions within very sparse interstellar or circumstellar clouds of dust and gas. Usually this occurs when a molecule becomes <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a>, often as the result of an interaction with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>. This positively charged molecule then draws in a nearby reactant by electrostatic attraction of the neutral molecule\'s electrons. Molecules can also be generated by reactions between neutral atoms and molecules, although this process is generally slower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas103_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-pnas103-15">[15]</a></sup> The dust plays a critical role of shielding the molecules from the ionizing effect of ultraviolet radiation emitted by stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_pais95_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-brown_pais95-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2008 analysis of <sup>12</sup>C/<sup>13</sup>C isotopic ratios of organic compounds found in the <a href="/wiki/Murchison_meteorite" title="Murchison meteorite">Murchison meteorite</a> indicates a non-terrestrial origin for these molecules rather than terrestrial contamination. Biologically relevant molecules identified so far include <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>, an RNA <a href="/wiki/Nucleobase" title="Nucleobase">nucleobase</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xanthine" title="Xanthine">xanthine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murch_base_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murch_base-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> These results demonstrate that many organic compounds which are components of life on Earth were already present in the early Solar System and may have played a key role in life\'s origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2009, NASA scientists identified one of the fundamental chemical building-blocks of life (the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>) in a comet for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2011, a report, based on <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> studies with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorites" title="Meteorites">meteorites</a> found on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, was published suggesting building blocks of <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> (<a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanine" title="Guanine">guanine</a> and related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecules" title="Organic molecules">organic molecules</a>) may have been formed extraterrestrially in <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callahan_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callahan-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steigerwald_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steigerwald-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNA_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNA-99">[99]</a></sup> In October 2011, scientists reported that <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a> contains complex <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> matter ("amorphous organic solids with a mixed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic" title="Aromatic">aromatic</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aliphatic" title="Aliphatic">aliphatic</a> structure") that could be created naturally, and rapidly, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stars" title="Stars">stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20111026_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20111026-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceDaily-20111026_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceDaily-20111026-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20111026_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20111026-102">[102]</a></sup> One of the scientists suggested that these complex organic compounds may have been related to the development of life on Earth and said that, "If this is the case, life on Earth may have had an easier time getting started as these organics can serve as basic ingredients for life."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20111026_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20111026-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2012, and in a world first, astronomers at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copenhagen_University" title="Copenhagen University">Copenhagen University</a> reported the detection of a specific sugar molecule, <a href="/wiki/Glycolaldehyde" title="Glycolaldehyde">glycolaldehyde</a>, in a distant star system. The molecule was found around the <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostellar</a> binary <i>IRAS 16293-2422</i>, which is located 400 light years from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20120829_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20120829-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20120829_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20120829-104">[104]</a></sup> Glycolaldehyde is needed to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribonucleic_acid" title="Ribonucleic acid">ribonucleic acid</a>, or <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, which is similar in function to <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>.  This finding suggests that complex organic molecules may form in stellar systems prior to the formation of planets, eventually arriving on young planets early in their formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2012, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> scientists reported that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs), subjected to <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium (ISM)</a> conditions, are transformed, through <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygenate" title="Oxygenate">oxygenation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxylation" title="Hydroxylation">hydroxylation</a>, to more complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">organics</a> &#8211; "a step along the path toward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a>, the raw materials of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, respectively".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120920_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120920-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJL-20120901_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJL-20120901-107">[107]</a></sup> Further, as a result of these transformations, the PAHs lose their <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopic signature</a> which could be one of the reasons "for the lack of PAH detection in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_ice" title="Interstellar ice">interstellar ice</a> <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust#Dust_grain_formation" title="Cosmic dust">grains</a>, particularly the outer regions of cold, dense clouds or the upper molecular layers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disks" title="Protoplanetary disks">protoplanetary disks</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120920_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120920-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJL-20120901_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-AJL-20120901-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">Atacama Large Millimeter Array</a> (ALMA Project) confirmed that researchers have discovered an important pair of prebiotic molecules in the icy particles in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_space" title="Interstellar space">interstellar space</a> (ISM). The chemicals, found in a giant cloud of gas about 25,000 light-years from Earth in ISM, may be a precursor to a key component of DNA and the other may have a role in the formation of an important <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a>. Researchers found a molecule called cyanomethanimine, which produces <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">adenine</a>, one of the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleobases" title="Nucleobases">nucleobases</a> that form the "rungs" in the ladder-like structure of DNA. The other molecule, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethanamine" title="Ethanamine">ethanamine</a>, is thought to play a role in forming <a href="/wiki/Alanine" title="Alanine">alanine</a>, one of the twenty amino acids in the genetic code. Previously, scientists thought such processes took place in the very tenuous gas between the stars. The new discoveries, however, suggest that the chemical formation sequences for these molecules occurred not in gas, but on the surfaces of ice grains in interstellar space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> NASA ALMA scientist Anthony Remijan stated that finding these molecules in an interstellar gas cloud means that important building blocks for DNA and amino acids can \'seed\' newly formed planets with the chemical precursors for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2013, a simulation experiment indicate that dipeptides (pairs of amino acids) that can be building blocks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a>, can be created in interstellar dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanders_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanders-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2014, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> announced a <a class="external text" href="http://www.astrochem.org/pahdb/" rel="nofollow">greatly upgraded database</a> for tracking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs) in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. According to scientists, more than 20% of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> in the universe may be associated with PAHs, possible <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">starting materials</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis#PAH_world_hypothesis" title="Abiogenesis">formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Life#Extraterrestrial_life" title="Life">life</a>. PAHs seem to have been formed shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, are widespread throughout the universe, and are associated with <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">new stars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exoplanets" title="Exoplanets">exoplanets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2015, NASA scientists reported that, for the first time, complex <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">cytosine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thymine" title="Thymine">thymine</a>, have been formed in the laboratory under <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a> conditions, using starting chemicals, such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrimidine" title="Pyrimidine">pyrimidine</a>, found in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. Pyrimidine, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs), the most carbon-rich chemical found in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, may have been formed in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giants</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">interstellar dust</a> and gas clouds, according to the scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150303_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150303-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2016, the Rosetta Mission team reported the presence of <a href="/wiki/Glycine" title="Glycine">glycine</a>, methylamine and ethylamine in the coma of 67P/Churyumov-Gerasimenko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> This, plus the detection of phosphorus, is consistent with the hypothesis that comets played a crucial role in the emergence of life on Earth.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_life">Extraterrestrial life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">Extraterrestrial life</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">chemistry of life</a> may have begun shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">13.8 billion years ago</a>, during a habitable epoch when the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> was only 10&#8211;17 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJA-2014October_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-IJA-2014October-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARXIV-20131202_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARXIV-20131202-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141202_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141202-116">[116]</a></sup>  According to the panspermia hypothesis, microscopic life&#8212;distributed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteoroids" title="Meteoroids">meteoroids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>&#8212;may exist throughout the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USRA-2010_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-USRA-2010-117">[117]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Earth is the only place in the universe known by humans to harbor life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-1990_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-1990-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astrobiology-2008_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astrobiology-2008-119">[119]</a></sup> The sheer number of planets in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy, however, may make it probable that life has arisen somewhere else in the galaxy and the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. It is generally agreed that the conditions required for the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> of intelligent life as we know it are probably exceedingly rare in the universe, while simultaneously noting that simple single-celled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a> may be more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb,_Stephen_2002_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb,_Stephen_2002-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planet</a> results from the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler mission</a> estimate 100&#8211;400 billion exoplanets, with over 3,500 as candidates or confirmed exoplanets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> On 4 November 2013, astronomers reported, based on <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler space mission</a> data, that there could be as many as 40 billion <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Earth-sized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planets" title="Extrasolar planets">planets</a> orbiting in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zones</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun-like" title="Sun-like">sun-like</a> stars and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_dwarf_stars" title="Red dwarf stars">red dwarf stars</a> within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy" title="Milky Way Galaxy">Milky Way Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131104_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131104-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20131031_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20131031-123">[123]</a></sup>  11 billion of these estimated planets may be orbiting sun-like stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes-20131104_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-20131104-124">[124]</a></sup> The nearest such planet may be 12 light-years away, according to the scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20131104_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131104-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PNAS-20131031_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20131031-123">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is estimated that space travel over cosmic distances would take an incredibly long time to an outside observer, and with vast amounts of energy required. However, some scientists hypothesize that <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_travel#Faster-than-light_travel" title="Interstellar travel">faster-than-light interstellar space travel</a> might be feasible.  This has been explored by NASA scientists since at least 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypotheses_on_extraterrestrial_sources_of_illnesses">Hypotheses on extraterrestrial sources of illnesses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hypotheses on extraterrestrial sources of illnesses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Hoyle and Wickramasinghe have speculated that several outbreaks of illnesses on Earth are of extraterrestrial origins, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a>, and certain outbreaks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mad_cow_disease" title="Mad cow disease">mad cow disease</a>.  For the 1918 flu pandemic they hypothesized that <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">cometary</a> dust brought the virus to Earth simultaneously at multiple locations&#8212;a view almost universally dismissed by experts on this pandemic. Hoyle also speculated that <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> came from outer space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne2008_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Byrne2008-126">[126]</a></sup> After Hoyle\'s death, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">The Lancet</a></i> published a <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_editor" title="Letter to the editor">letter to the editor</a> from Wickramasinghe and two of his colleagues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> in which they hypothesized that the <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a> that causes <a href="/wiki/Severe_acute_respiratory_syndrome" title="Severe acute respiratory syndrome">severe acute respiratory syndrome</a> (SARS) could be extraterrestrial in origin and not originated from chickens. <i>The Lancet</i> subsequently published three responses to this letter, showing that the hypothesis was not evidence-based, and casting doubts on the quality of the experiments referenced by Wickramasinghe in his letter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> A 2008 encyclopedia notes that "Like other claims linking terrestrial disease to extraterrestrial pathogens, this proposal was rejected by the greater research community."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne2008_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Byrne2008-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2016, Jiangwen Qu of the Department of Infectious Disease Control in China presented a statistical study suggesting that "extremes of sunspot activity to within plus or minus 1 &#8201;year may precipitate influenza pandemics."  He discussed possible mechanisms of epidemic initiation and early spread, including speculation on primary causation by externally derived viral variants from space via cometary dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Case_studies">Case studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Case studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorite</a> originating from <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ALH84001" title="ALH84001">ALH84001</a> was shown in 1996 to contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microscopic" title="Microscopic">microscopic</a> structures resembling small terrestrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanobacteria" title="Nanobacteria">nanobacteria</a>. When the discovery was announced, many immediately conjectured that these were <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> and were the first evidence of <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a> &#8212; making headlines around the world. Public interest soon started to dwindle as most experts started to agree that these structures were not indicative of life, but could instead be formed abiotically from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecules" title="Organic molecules">organic molecules</a>. However, in November 2009, a team of scientists at <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>, including David McKay, reasserted that there was "strong evidence that life may have existed on ancient Mars", after having reexamined the meteorite and finding <a href="/wiki/Magnetite#Biological_occurrences" title="Magnetite">magnetite</a> crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></li>\n<li>On May 11, 2001, two researchers from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Naples_Federico_II" title="University of Naples Federico II">University of Naples</a> claimed to have found viable extraterrestrial bacteria inside a meteorite. Geologist Bruno D\'Argenio and molecular biologist Giuseppe Geraci claim the bacteria were wedged inside the crystal structure of minerals, but were resurrected when a sample of the rock was placed in a culture medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></li>\n<li>An Indian and British team of researchers led by Chandra Wickramasinghe reported on 2001 that air samples over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyderabad,_India" title="Hyderabad, India">Hyderabad, India</a>, gathered from the stratosphere by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> (ISRO) on Jan 21, 2001, contained clumps of living cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Wickramasinghe calls this "unambiguous evidence for the presence of clumps of living cells in air samples from as high as 41&#160;km, above which no air from lower down would normally be transported".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam_Wick_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciam_Wick-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Narlikar_2003_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Narlikar_2003-139">[139]</a></sup> Two bacterial and one fungal species were later independently isolated from these filters which were identified as <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bacillus_simplex&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bacillus simplex (page does not exist)">Bacillus simplex</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Staphylococcus_pasteuri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Staphylococcus pasteuri (page does not exist)">Staphylococcus pasteuri</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Engyodontium_album&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Engyodontium album (page does not exist)">Engyodontium album</a></i> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wainwright,_2003_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wainwright,_2003-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Pushkar Ganesh Vaidya from the Indian Astrobiology Research Centre reported in 2009 that "the three microorganisms captured during the balloon experiment do not exhibit any distinct adaptations expected to be seen in microorganisms occupying a cometary niche".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In 2005 an improved experiment was conducted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">ISRO</a>. On April 20, 2005, air samples were collected from the upper atmosphere at altitudes ranging from 20&#160;km to more than 40&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The samples were tested at two labs in India. The labs found 12 bacterial and 6 different fungal species in these samples. The fungi were <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium_decumbens" title="Penicillium decumbens">Penicillium decumbens</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cladosporium_cladosporioides" title="Cladosporium cladosporioides">Cladosporium cladosporioides</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Alternaria" title="Alternaria">Alternaria</a> sp.</i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tilletiopsis_albescens&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tilletiopsis albescens (page does not exist)">Tilletiopsis albescens</a></i>. Out of the 12 bacterial samples, three were identified as new species and named <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Janibacter_hoylei&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Janibacter hoylei (page does not exist)">Janibacter hoylei</a></i> (after <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a>), <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bacillus_isronensis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bacillus isronensis (page does not exist)">Bacillus isronensis</a></i> (named after ISRO) and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bacillus_aryabhattai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bacillus aryabhattai (page does not exist)">Bacillus aryabhattai</a></i> (named after the ancient Indian mathematician, <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>). These three new species showed that they were more resistant to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_radiation" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a> than similar bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<dl><dd>Some other researchers have retrieved bacteria from the stratosphere since the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Atmospheric sampling by NASA in 2010 before and after hurricanes, collected 314 different types of bacteria; the study suggests that large-scale convection during tropical storms and hurricanes can then carry this material from the surface higher up into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hurricanes_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-hurricanes-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<ul><li>Another proposed mechanism of spores in the stratosphere is lifting by weather and Earth magnetism up to the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> into low Earth orbit, where Russian astronauts retrieved <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> from a known sterile exterior surface of the International Space Station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russia_ISS_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russia_ISS-36">[36]</a></sup> The Russian scientists then also speculated the possibility "that common terrestrial bacteria are constantly being resupplied from space."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russia_ISS_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russia_ISS-36">[36]</a></sup></li>\n<li>In 2013, Dale Warren Griffin, a microbiologist working at the United States Geological Survey noted that viruses are the most numerous entities on Earth. Griffin speculates that viruses evolved in comets and on other planets and moons may be pathogenic to humans, so he proposed to also look for viruses on moons and planets of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astro-20130814_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astro-20130814-150">[150]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hoaxes">Hoaxes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Panspermia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hoaxes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A separate fragment of the <a href="/wiki/Orgueil_(meteorite)" title="Orgueil (meteorite)">Orgueil</a> meteorite (kept in a sealed glass jar since its discovery) was found in 1965 to have a seed capsule embedded in it, whilst the original glassy layer on the outside remained undisturbed. Despite great initial excitement, the seed was found to be that of a European <a href="/wiki/Juncaceae" title="Juncaceae">Juncaceae</a> or Rush plant that had been glued into the fragment and camouflaged using <a href="/wiki/Coal_dust" title="Coal dust">coal dust</a>. The outer "fusion layer" was in fact glue. Whilst the perpetrator of this hoax is unknown, it is thought that they sought to influence the 19th century debate on <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a> &#8212;
